ol.enhanced-list ol.enhanced-list .list-item {
    counter-increment: list-counter-alpha;
}

ol.enhanced-list ol.enhanced-list .list-marker::before {
    content: counter(list-counter-alpha, lower-alpha);
}

/* Level 3: Lowercase roman numerals */

    counter-reset: list-counter-roman;
}

    counter-increment: list-counter-roman;
}

ol.enhanced-list ol.enhanced-list ol.enhanced-list .list-marker::before {
    content: counter(list-counter-roman, lower-roman);
}

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="mbio-140">MBIO 140<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mbio-140" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-d16fb424.jpeg" alt="img-1.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-a00396fd.jpeg" alt="img-2.jpeg"></p>
<p>"...THE ROLE OF THE INFINITELY SMALL IN NATURE IS</p>
<p>INFINITELY LARGE"... Louis Pasteur</p>
<p>Welcome to microbiologythe study of microorganisms.</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id=""><span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"></span></h1></div>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Microorganisms are single-celled microscopic organisms and viruses, which are microscopic but not cellular.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-is-microbiology-all-about">What is microbiology all about?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-is-microbiology-all-about" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Microbiology is about cells and how they work, especially the bacteria, a large group of cells of enormous basic and practical importance (Figure 1.1).</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-bafcfe70.jpeg" alt="img-3.jpeg"></p>
<p>Figure 1.1 Microorganisms. (a, b) A single microbial cell can have an independent existence. Shown are photomicrographs of phototrophic (photosynthetic) microorganisms called (a) purple bacteria and (b) cyanobacteria and (d) bioluminescent (light-emitting) cells of the bacterium <em>Photobacterium leiognathi</em> grown in laboratory culture. One milliliter of water from the lake (c) or one colony from the plate (d) contains more than 1 billion (<span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>10</mn><mn>9</mn></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">10^{9}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8141em;"></span><span class="mord">1</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">0</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8141em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">9</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span>) individual cells.</p>
<ul>
<li>Microbiology is about diversity and evolution, about how different kinds of microorganisms arose and why.</li>
<li>It is about what microorganisms do in the world at large, in soils and waters, in the human body, and in animals and plants.</li>
<li>One way or another, microorganisms affect all other life forms on Earth, and thus we may think of microbiology as the foundation of the biological sciences.</li>
<li>Microorganisms differ from the cells of macroorganisms. The cells of macroorganisms such as plants and animals are unable to live alone in nature and exist only as parts of multicellular structures, such as the organ systems of animals or the leaves of leafy plants.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>By contrast, most microorganisms can carry out their life processes of growth, energy generation, and reproduction independently of other cells.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-introduction-to-microbiology">1. INTRODUCTION TO MICROBIOLOGY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-introduction-to-microbiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-1-microbiology">1.1 Microbiology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-1-microbiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The science of microbiology revolves around two themes:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="1-understanding-basic-life-processes-and">(1) Understanding basic life processes, and<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-understanding-basic-life-processes-and" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<ul>
<li>As a basic biological science, microbiology uses and develops tools for probing the fundamental processes of life.</li>
<li>Scientists have been able to gain a sophisticated understanding of the chemical and physical basis of life from studies of microorganisms because microbial cells share many characteristics with cells of multicellular organisms; indeed, all cells have much in common.</li>
<li>Moreover, microbial cells can grow to extremely high densities in laboratory culture, making them readily amenable to biochemical and genetic study.</li>
<li>These features make microorganisms excellent models for understanding cellular processes in multicellular organisms, including humans</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="2-applying-our-understanding-of-microbiology-for-the-benefit-of-humankind">(2) Applying our understanding of microbiology for the benefit of humankind.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-applying-our-understanding-of-microbiology-for-the-benefit-of-humankind" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<p>As an applied biological science, microbiology deals with many important practical problems in medicine, agriculture, and industry. For example:</p>
<ul>
<li>Most animal and plant diseases are caused by microorganisms.</li>
<li>Microorganisms play major roles as agents of soil fertility and in supporting domestic animal production.</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>3</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Many large-scale industrial processes, such as the production of antibiotics and human proteins, rely heavily on microorganisms. Thus both the detrimental and the beneficial aspects of microorganisms affect the everyday lives of humans.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-importance-of-microorganisms">The Importance of Microorganisms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-importance-of-microorganisms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>In this book we will see that microorganisms play central roles in both human activities and the web of life on Earth.</li>
<li>Although microorganisms are the smallest forms of life, collectively they constitute the largest mass of living material on Earth and carry out many chemical processes necessary for other organisms.</li>
<li>In the absence of microorganisms, other life forms would never have arisen and could not now be sustained. Indeed, the very oxygen we breathe is the result of past microbial activity (Figure 1.b).</li>
<li>Moreover, we will see how humans, plants, and animals are intimately tied to microbial activities for the recycling of key nutrients and for degrading organic matter.</li>
<li>No other life forms are as important as microorganisms for the support and maintenance of life on Earth.</li>
<li>Microorganisms existed on Earth for billions of years before plants and animals appeared, and we will see in later chapters that the diversity of microbial life far exceeds that of the plants and animals.</li>
<li>This huge diversity accounts for some of the spectacular properties of microorganisms. For example, we will see how microorganisms can live in places unsuitable for other organisms and how the diverse physiological capacities of microorganisms rank them as Earth's premier chemists.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="1-2-pathways-of-discovery-in-microbiology">1.2. PATHWAYS OF DISCOVERY IN MICROBIOLOGY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-2-pathways-of-discovery-in-microbiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Like any science, microbiology owes much to its past. Although able to claim early roots, the science of microbiology didn't really develop until the nineteenth century. Since that time, the field has exploded and spawned several new but related fields. We retrace these pathways of discovery now.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="the-historical-roots-of-microbiology-hooke-van-leeuwenhoek-and-cohn">The Historical Roots of Microbiology: Hooke, van Leeuwenhoek, and Cohn<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-historical-roots-of-microbiology-hooke-van-leeuwenhoek-and-cohn" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Although the existence of creatures too small to be seen with the naked eye had long been suspected, their discovery was linked to the invention of the microscope.</li>
<li>Robert Hooke (16351703), an English mathematician and natural historian, was also an excellent microscopist. In his famous book Micrographia (1665), the first book devoted to microscopic observations, Hooke illustrated, among many other things, the fruiting structures of molds (Figure 1.9). This was the first known description of microorganisms.</li>
<li>The first person to see bacteria was the Dutch draper and amateur microscope builder Antoni van Leeuwenhoek (16321723).</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-ca906e72.jpeg" alt="img-4.jpeg">
Figure 1.9: Robert Hooke and early microscopy. (a) A drawing of the microscope used by Robert Hooke in 1664. This drawing, published in Micrographia in 1655, is the first description of a microorganism. The organism is a bluish-colored mold growing on the surface of leather. The round structures (sporangia) contain spores of the mold.</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>In 1684, van Leeuwenhoek, who was aware of the work of Hooke, used extremely simple microscopes of his own construction (Figure 1.10) to examine the microbial content of a variety of natural substances</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Van Leeuwenhoek's microscopes were crude by today's standards, but by careful manipulation and focusing he was able to see bacteria, microorganisms considerably smaller than molds. He discovered bacteria in 1676 while studying pepperwater infusions.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>He reported his observations in a series of letters to the prestigious Royal Society of London, which published them in 1684 in English translation. Drawings of some of van Leeuwenhoek's wee animalcules, as he referred to them, are shown in Figure 1.10b.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>As years went by, van Leeuwenhoek's observations were confirmed by others, but progress in understanding the nature and importance of these tiny organisms remained slow for nearly the next 150 years.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Only in the nineteenth century did improved microscopes become widely distributed, and about this time the extent and nature of microbial life forms became more apparent.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>In the mid- to late nineteenth century major advances were made in the new science of microbiology, primarily because of the attention that was given to two major questions that pervaded biology and medicine at the time: (1) does spontaneous generation occur and (2) what is the nature of infectious disease.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Answers to these penetrating questions emerged from the work of two giants in the fledgling field of microbiology: the French chemist Louis Pasteur and the German physician Robert Koch. But before we explore their work, let us briefly consider the groundbreaking work of a German botanist, Ferdinand Cohn, a contemporary of Pasteur and Koch and the founder of the field we now call bacteriology</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-b6566672.jpeg" alt="img-5.jpeg">
Figure 1.10 The van Leeuwenhoek microscope. (a) A replica of van Leeuwenhoek's microscope. The lens is mounted in the brass plate adjacent to the tip of the adjustable focusing screw. (b) Antoni van Leeuwenhoek's drawings of bacteria, published in 1684. Even from these relatively crude drawings we can recognize several shapes of common bacteria: A, C, F, and G, rod shaped; E, spherical or coccus-shaped; H, cocci packets. (c) Photomicrograph of a human blood smear taken through a van Leeuwenhoek microscope. Red blood cells are clearly apparent. A single red blood cell is about  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>6</mn><mi></mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">6\mu \mathrm{m}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8389em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord">6</span><span class="mord mathnormal"></span><span class="mord mathrm">m</span></span></span></span></span>  in diameter.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-5da1e92d.jpeg" alt="img-6.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-fa1e9d6a.jpeg" alt="img-7.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="ferdinand-cohn-and-the-science-of-bacteriology">Ferdinand Cohn and the Science of Bacteriology:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ferdinand-cohn-and-the-science-of-bacteriology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Ferdinand Cohn (1828-1898) was born in Poland. He was trained as a botanist and became an excellent microscopist.</li>
<li>His interests in microscopy naturally led him to the study of unicellular plantsthe algaeand later to photosynthetic bacteria.</li>
<li>Cohn believed that all bacteria, even those lacking photosynthetic pigments, were members of the plant kingdom, and his microscopic studies gradually drifted away from plants and algae to bacteria, including the large sulfur bacterium Beggiatoa (Figure 1.11).</li>
<li>Cohn was particularly interested in heat resistance in bacteria, which led him to discover the important group of bacteria that form endospores.</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id=""><span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"></span></h1></div>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>We now know that bacterial endospores are extremely heat resistant. Cohn described the life cycle of the endospore-forming bacterium Bacillus (vegetative cell  endospore  vegetative cell) and discovered that vegetative cells of Bacillus but not their endospores were killed by boiling.</li>
<li>Indeed, Cohns discovery of endospores helped explain why his contemporaries, such as the Irish scientist John Tyndall, had found boiling to be an unreliable means of preventing fluid infusions from supporting microbial growth.</li>
<li>Cohn laid the ground work for a system of bacterial classification, including an early attempt to define the nature of a bacterial species, an issue still unresolved today</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-cd5eebf0.jpeg" alt="img-8.jpeg">
Figure 1.11. Drawing by Ferdinand Cohn made in 1866 of the large filamentous sulfur-oxidizing bacterium Beggiatoa mirabilis.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="minireview">MiniReview<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#minireview" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Robert Hooke was the first to describe microorganisms, and Antoni van Leeuwenhoek was the first to describe bacteria. Ferdinand Cohn founded the field of bacteriology and discovered bacterial endospores</p>
<ol>
<li>What prevented the science of microbiology from developing before the era of van Leeuwenhoek?</li>
<li>What major discovery emerged from Cohns study of heat resistance in microorganisms?</li>
</ol>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="pasteur-and-the-defeat-of-spontaneous-generation">Pasteur and the Defeat of Spontaneous Generation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pasteur-and-the-defeat-of-spontaneous-generation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>The mid- to late nineteenth century saw the science of microbiology blossom.</li>
<li>The concept of spontaneous generation was crushed and the science of pure culture microbiology emerged.</li>
<li>Several scientific giants emerged in this era, and the first was the Frenchman Louis Pasteur (18221895), a contemporary of Cohn. Pasteur was trained as a chemist</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="fermentations">Fermentations<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fermentations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>At the invitation of a local industrialist who was having problems making alcohol by the fermentation of beets, Pasteur began a detailed study of the mechanism of the alcoholic fermentation, at that time thought to be a strictly chemical process.</li>
<li>The yeast cells in the fermenting broth were thought to be a complex chemical substance and a result, rather than a catalyst, of the fermentation.</li>
<li>Microscopic observations and other simple but rigorous experiments convinced Pasteur that the alcoholic fermentation was catalyzed by living yeast cells. Indeed, in Pasteurs own words: . . . fermentation is associated with the life and structural integrity of the cells and not with their death and decay.</li>
<li>From this foundation, Pasteur began a series of classic experiments on spontaneous generation, experiments that are forever linked to his name and to the science of microbiology.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="spontaneous-generation">Spontaneous Generation:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#spontaneous-generation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The concept of spontaneous generation had existed since biblical times.</li>
<li>The basic idea of spontaneous generation can easily be understood. For example, if food is allowed to stand for some time, it putrefies.</li>
<li>When the putrefied material is examined microscopically, it is found to be teeming with bacteria and perhaps even higher organisms such as maggots and worms.</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>9</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Where do these organisms that are not apparent in the fresh food come from? Some people said they developed from seeds or germs that entered the food from air. Others said they arose spontaneously from nonliving materials, that is, spontaneous generation. Who was right?</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Keen insight was necessary to solve this controversy, and this was exactly the kind of problem that appealed to Louis Pasteur.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Pasteur was a powerful opponent of spontaneous generation. Following his discoveries about fermentation.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Pasteur showed that microorganisms closely resembling those observed in putrefying materials could be found in air.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Pasteur concluded that the organisms found in putrefying materials originated from microorganisms present in the air and on the surfaces of the containers that held the materials.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>He postulated that cells are constantly being deposited on all objects and that they grow when conditions are favorable. Furthermore, Pasteur reasoned that if food were treated in such a way as to destroy all living organisms contaminating it, that is, if it were rendered sterile and then protected from further contamination, it should not putrefy.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Pasteur used heat to eliminate contaminants. Other workers had shown that when a nutrient solution was sealed in a glass flask and heated to boiling for several minutes, it did not support microbial growth (of course, only if endospores were not present; see discussion of Cohn). Killing all the bacteria or other microorganisms in or on objects is a process we now call sterilization.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Proponents of spontaneous generation criticized such experiments by declaring that "fresh air" was necessary for the phenomenon to occur. Boiling, so they claimed, in</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)
10</p>
<p>some way affected the air in the sealed flask so that it could no longer support spontaneous generation.</p>
<ul>
<li>In 1864 Pasteur countered this objection simply and brilliantly by constructing a swan-necked flask, now called a Pasteur flask (Figure 1.13).</li>
<li>In such a flask nutrient solutions could be heated to boiling and sterilized. However, after the flask was cooled, air was allowed to reenter, but bends in the neck (the "swan neck" design) prevented particulate matter (containing microorganisms) from entering the main body of the flask and causing putrefaction.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-4a934ee2.jpeg" alt="img-9.jpeg">
Figure 1.13. The defeat of spontaneous generation: Pasteur's experiment with the swannecked flask. (a) Sterilizing the contents of the flask. (b) If the flask remained upright, no microbial growth occurred. (c) If microorganisms trapped in the neck reached the sterile liquid, microbial growth ensued</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id=""><span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"></span></h1></div>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Broth sterilized in a Pasteur flask did not putrefy, and microorganisms never appeared in the flask as long as the neck did not contact the sterile liquid.</li>
<li>If, however, the flask was tipped to allow the sterile liquid to contact the contaminated neck of the flask (Figure 1.13c), putrefaction occurred and the liquid soon teemed with microorganisms.</li>
<li>This simple experiment effectively settled the controversy surrounding spontaneous generation, and the science of microbiology was able to move ahead on firm footing. Incidentally,</li>
<li>Pasteurs work also led to the development of effective sterilization procedures that were eventually refined and carried over into both basic and applied microbiological research.</li>
<li>Food science also owes a debt to Pasteur, as his principles are applied today in the canning and preservation of milk and other foods (pasteurization).</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="other-accomplishments-of-louis-pasteur">Other Accomplishments of Louis Pasteur:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#other-accomplishments-of-louis-pasteur" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Pasteur went on to many other triumphs in microbiology and medicine beyond his seminal work on spontaneous generation.</li>
<li>Some highlights include his development of vaccines for the diseases anthrax, fowl cholera, and rabies during a very scientifically productive period in his life from 1880 to 1890.</li>
<li>Pasteurs work on rabies was his most famous success, culminating in July of 1885 with the first administration of a rabies vaccine to a human, a young French boy named Joseph Meister who had been bitten by a rabid dog. In those days, a bite from a rabid animal was akin to a death sentence.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>News of the success of Meister's vaccination, and that of a young shepherd boy, Jean Baptiste Jupille (Figure 1.14a), administered shortly thereafter, spread quickly, and within a year nearly 2500 people had come to Paris to be treated with Pasteur's rabies vaccine.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-4cc6a630.jpeg" alt="img-10.jpeg">
Figure 1.14. Louis Pasteur and symbols of his contributions to microbiology. (a) A French 5-franc note.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-08432240.jpeg" alt="img-11.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="minireview">MiniReview<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#minireview" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Louis Pasteur is best remembered for his ingenious experiments showing that living organisms were not spontaneously generated from nonliving matter. Pasteur's work in this area led to many of the basic techniques central to the science of microbiology, including the concept and practice of sterilization</p>
<ol>
<li>Define the term sterile.</li>
<li>How did Pasteur's swan-neck flask experiment show that the concept of spontaneous generation was invalid?</li>
</ol>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id=""><span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"></span></h1></div>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Robert Koch (18431910), Infectious Disease, and the Rise of Pure Culture</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="microbiology">Microbiology:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#microbiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Proof that microorganisms could cause disease provided perhaps the greatest impetus for the development of the science of microbiology.</li>
<li>Even in the sixteenth century it was thought that something that induced a disease could be transmitted from a diseased person to a healthy person.</li>
<li>After the discovery of microorganisms, it was widely believed that they were responsible, but definitive proof was lacking.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="388d2847">The Germ Theory of Disease and Kochs Postulates:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#388d2847" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>In his early work Koch studied anthrax, a disease of cattle and occasionally of humans.</li>
<li>Anthrax is caused by an endospore-forming bacterium called <em>Bacillus anthracis</em>.</li>
<li>By careful microscopy and by using special stains, Koch established that the bacteria were always present in the blood of an animal that was succumbing to the disease.</li>
<li>However, Koch reasoned that mere association of the bacterium with the disease was not proof that it actually caused the disease. Instead, the bacterium might be a result of the disease. How could cause and effect be linked? With anthrax Koch sensed an opportunity to study cause and effect experimentally, and his results formed the standard by which infectious diseases have been studied ever since.</li>
<li>Koch used mice as experimental animals. Using all of the proper controls, Koch demonstrated that when a small amount of blood from a diseased mouse was injected into a healthy mouse, the latter quickly developed anthrax.</li>
<li>He took blood from this second animal, injected it into another, and again obtained the characteristic disease symptoms.</li>
<li>However, Koch carried this experiment a critically important step further. He discovered that the anthrax bacteria could be grown in nutrient fluids outside the animal body and</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>that even after many transfers in laboratory culture, the bacteria still caused the disease when inoculated into a healthy animal.</p>
<ul>
<li>On the basis of these and related experiments carried out in his seminal work on the causative agent of tuberculosis, Koch formulated a set of rigorous criteria, now known as <strong>Kochs postulates</strong>, for definitively linking a specific microorganism to a specific disease:</li>
</ul>
<ol>
<li>The disease-causing organism must always be present in animals suffering from the disease and should not be present in healthy animals.</li>
<li>The organism must be cultivated in a pure culture away from the animal body</li>
<li>The isolated organism must cause the disease when inoculated into a healthy susceptible animal</li>
<li>The organism must be reisolated from these experimental animals and cultured again in the laboratory, after which it should still be the same as the original organism</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Kochs postulates are summarized in Figure 1.15. Kochs postulates were a monumental step forward in the study of infectious diseases.</li>
<li>The postulates not only offered a means for linking the cause and effect of an infectious disease, but also stressed the importance of laboratory culture of the putative infectious agent. With these postulates as a guide, Koch, his students, and those that followed them discovered the causative agents of most of the important infectious diseases of humans and other animals.</li>
<li>These discoveries led to the development of successful treatments for the prevention and cure of many of these diseases, thereby greatly improving the scientific basis of clinical medicine and human health and welfare (Figure 1.8)</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-0dc7e883.jpeg" alt="img-12.jpeg">
Figure 1.15 Koch's postulates for proving that a specific microorganism causes a specific disease. Note that following isolation of a pure culture of the suspected pathogen, a laboratory culture of the organism should both initiate the disease and be recovered from the diseased animal. Establishing the correct conditions for growing the pathogen is essential, otherwise it will be missed.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="the-modern-era-of-microbiology">The Modern Era of Microbiology:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-modern-era-of-microbiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>In the middle to latter part of the twentieth century, basic and applied microbiology worked hand in hand to usher in the current era of molecular microbiology. Figure 1.16 depicts some of the landmarks in microbiology in the past 65 years.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="1-3-general-characteristics-of-microorganisms">1.3. General Characteristics of Microorganisms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-3-general-characteristics-of-microorganisms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Prokaryotes: No nucleus and organelles</li>
<li>Eukaryotes: Membrane bound nucleus and organelles</li>
<li>Acellular agents (Viruses): Genomes contain either DNA or RNA; newer agent is proteinaceous</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-b58e8ce8.jpeg" alt="img-14.jpeg"></p>
<p>Prokaryotic and Eukaryotic cell</p>
<p>18</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="size-of-microorganisms">Size of microorganisms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#size-of-microorganisms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Microorganisms vary in size ranging from 10 nm (nanometers) to 100 mu (micrometers):</p>
<ul>
<li>Viruses in <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="bold">n</mi><mi mathvariant="bold">m</mi></mrow><mo>=</mo><msup><mn>10</mn><mrow><mo></mo><mn>9</mn></mrow></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathbf{nm} = 10^{-9}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.4444em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathbf">nm</span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8141em;"></span><span class="mord">1</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">0</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8141em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight"></span><span class="mord mtight">9</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span> m (meter)</li>
<li>Bacteria in <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="bold">u</mi><mi mathvariant="bold">m</mi></mrow><mo>=</mo><msup><mn>10</mn><mrow><mo></mo><mn>6</mn></mrow></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathbf{um} = 10^{-6}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.4444em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathbf">um</span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8141em;"></span><span class="mord">1</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">0</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8141em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight"></span><span class="mord mtight">6</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span> m</li>
<li>Helminths in <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="bold">m</mi><mi mathvariant="bold">m</mi></mrow><mo>=</mo><msup><mn>10</mn><mrow><mo></mo><mn>3</mn></mrow></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathbf{mm} = 10^{-3}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.4444em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathbf">mm</span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8141em;"></span><span class="mord">1</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">0</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8141em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight"></span><span class="mord mtight">3</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span> m</li>
</ul>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="classification-of-microorganisms">Classification of microorganisms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#classification-of-microorganisms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>System for organizing, classifying, and naming living microorganisms Primary concerns of taxonomy are classification, nomenclature, and identification.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="classification">Classification:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#classification" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The Domain system was developed by Dr. Woese. The basis of the Domain system is the rRNA sequence information.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-96bee054.jpeg" alt="img-16.jpeg"></p>
<p>Classification of microorganisms</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-d00a84a7.jpeg" alt="img-17.jpeg"></p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="nomenclature">Nomenclature:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nomenclature" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Learn Binomial (scientific) nomenclature</li>
<li>Genus  Saccharomyces, always capitalized</li>
<li>species - cerevisiae, lowercase</li>
<li>Both italicized or underlined: Saccharomyces cerevisiae or Saccharomyces cerevisiae</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="identification">Identification:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#identification" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The process of microbial identification and placing them in a taxonomic scheme includes:</p>
<ol>
<li>Microscopic morphology and colony appearance</li>
<li>Physiological/biochemical characteristics</li>
<li>Chemical analysis</li>
<li>Serological analysis</li>
<li>Genetic and molecular analysis</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>G + C base composition</li>
<li>DNA analysis using genetic probes</li>
<li>Nucleic acid sequencing and rRNA analysis</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="85fabeb">Bacterial Taxonomy Based on Bergeys Manual:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#85fabeb" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>classification based on genetic information  phylogenetic</li>
<li>two domains: Archaea and Bacteria</li>
<li>five major subgroups with 25 different phyla</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="major-groups-of-microorganisms">Major groups of Microorganisms:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#major-groups-of-microorganisms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Bacteria: Bacteriology</li>
<li>Fungi: Mycology</li>
<li>Algae: Phycology</li>
<li>Virus: Virology</li>
<li>Protozoa: Protozoology</li>
</ol>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="2-3-bacterial-growth">2.3. Bacterial Growth<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-3-bacterial-growth" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Culture: Increase in the population of cells</p>
<p>Generation time: The time cell takes to divide (double) is called</p>
<p>Reproduction: Binary Fission</p>
<ul>
<li>Division exactly in half</li>
<li>Most common means of bacterial reproduction</li>
<li>Forming two equal size progeny</li>
<li>Genetically identical offspring</li>
<li>Cells divide in a geometric progression doubling cell number</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-e7855e78.jpeg" alt="img-24.jpeg"></p>
<p>Binary Fission: Doubling time is the unit of measurement of microbial growth</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>2.4. Bacterial Culture Growth</p>
<p>Growth of culture goes through four phases with time:</p>
<ol>
<li><strong>Lag phase:</strong></li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Organisms are adapting to the environment</li>
<li>Synthesizing DNA, ribosomes and enzymes in order to breakdown nutrients, and to be used for growth</li>
<li>Little or no division</li>
</ul>
<ol start="2">
<li><strong>Log or Logarithmic phase</strong></li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Division is at a constant rate</li>
</ul>
<ol start="3">
<li><strong>Stationary phase:</strong></li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Dying and dividing organisms are at an equilibrium</li>
<li>Death is due to reduced nutrients, pH changes, toxic waste and reduced oxygen</li>
<li>Cells are smaller and have fewer ribosomes</li>
</ul>
<ol start="4">
<li><strong>Death or Decline phase</strong></li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>The population is dying in a geometric fashion so there are more deaths than new cells</li>
<li>Deaths are due to severe reduced nutrients, pH changes, toxic waste and reduced oxygen</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-f430eba3.jpeg" alt="img-25.jpeg">
Bacterial Growth Curve</p>

5
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="2-5-factors-influencing-bacterial-growth">2.5. Factors Influencing Bacterial Growth<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-5-factors-influencing-bacterial-growth" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Nutrition, Temperature, Oxygen, Salinity, pH, Pressure, Radiation</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nutrition">Nutrition:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nutrition" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="source-of-energy">Source of Energy:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#source-of-energy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Bacteria are found in almost every environment because they can use widely different energy sources.</li>
<li>Based on their energy source bacteria can be grouped into 4 major types</li>
<li>Photosynthetic Bacteria: (1) Photoautotrophs and (2) Photoheterotrophs</li>
<li>Chemosynthetic Bacteria: (3) Chemoautotrophs and (4) Chemoheterotrophs</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="basic-bacterial-requirements">Basic bacterial requirements:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#basic-bacterial-requirements" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Water:</strong> Used to dissolve materials to be transported across the cytoplasmic membrane</p>
<p><strong>Carbon:</strong> required for the construction of all organic molecules</p>
<ul>
<li>Autotrophs: use inorganic carbon (CO) as their carbon source</li>
<li>Heterotrophs: use organic carbon</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Nitrogen:</strong> Obtained from:</p>
<ul>
<li>Inorganic source: e.g. Nitrogen gas (N), Nitrate (NO), Nitrite (NO), and Ammonia (NH)</li>
<li>Organic source: e.g. Proteins, broken down to amino acids</li>
<li>Many organisms use nitrogen gas by nitrogen fixation to produce ammonia</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Other nutrients:</strong> Required in small amounts such as Iron, Sulfur, and Phosphorus</p>
<p>Nutritional Patterns:</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Table 27.1 Major Nutritional Modes</th><th></th><th></th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Mode of Nutrition</td><td>Energy Source</td><td>Carbon Source</td><td>Types of Organisms</td></tr><tr><td>Autotroph</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Photo-autotroph</td><td>Light</td><td>CO2</td><td>Photosynthetic prokaryotes, including cyanobacteria; plants; certain protists (algae)</td></tr><tr><td>Chemo-autotroph</td><td>Inorganic chemicals</td><td>CO2</td><td>Certain prokaryotes (for example, Sulfolobus)</td></tr><tr><td>Heterotroph</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Photo-heterotroph</td><td>Light</td><td>Organic com-pounds</td><td>Certain prokaryotes</td></tr><tr><td>Chemo-heterotroph</td><td>Organic com-pounds</td><td>Organic com-pounds</td><td>Many prokaryotes and protists; fungi; animals; some parasitic plants</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Copyright  Pearson Education, Inc., publishing as Benjamin Cummings.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="bacterial-temperature-requirements">Bacterial Temperature Requirements<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#bacterial-temperature-requirements" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Psychrophiles:</strong> Some exist below 0 C if liquid water is available eg. Oceans, refrigerators, and freezers</p>
<p><strong>Mesophiles:</strong> Most human flora and pathogens.</p>
<p><strong>Thermophiles:</strong> Hot springs, effluents from Laundromat, and deep ocean thermal vents</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-0d23c9a4.jpeg" alt="img-26.jpeg"></p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="oxygen">OXYGEN<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#oxygen" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Required for aerobic respiration and energy production</li>
<li>Organisms are classified according to their gaseous requirements</li>
</ul>
<ol>
<li>Obligate aerobes</li>
<li>Facultative anaerobes</li>
<li>Obligate anaerobes</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-5e3efb38.jpeg" alt="img-27.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="salinity">Salinity<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#salinity" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><strong>Halophiles</strong>: Bacteria that specifically require NaCl for growth</p>
<p><strong>Moderates Halophiles</strong>:</p>
<ul>
<li>Grow best at 3% NaCl solution</li>
<li>Many ocean dwelling bacteria</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Extreme Halophiles</strong>:</p>
<ul>
<li>Grow well at NaCl concentrations of greater than 15% e.g salt lakes, pickle barrels</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-206536b5.jpeg" alt="img-28.jpeg"></p>
<p>Halophiles growing within salt lakes often turn the water pink</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="bacterial-ph-requirements">Bacterial pH Requirements<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#bacterial-ph-requirements" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Microbes have different optimum pH requirements:</p>
<p><strong>Acidophiles:</strong> Some bacteria can grow in acid substrates</p>
<p><strong>Neutrophiles:</strong> most microbes prefer a pH near neutrality</p>
<p><strong>Alkalinophiles:</strong> Microbes which can grow in very alkaline substrates</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-a7bbaca0.jpeg" alt="img-29.jpeg">
Bacterial pH requirements</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-6-control-of-microbial-growth">2.6. Control of microbial growth<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-6-control-of-microbial-growth" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Sterilization:</strong> Removal or destruction of all microbial life forms</p>
<p><strong>Physical Methods of Microbial growth Control:</strong></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-heat">1. Heat:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-heat" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p><strong>Moist heat:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Coagulation (denaturing) of proteins</li>
<li>Hydrogen bonds are broken</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-1-boiling">1.1. Boiling:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-1-boiling" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Not always effective</li>
<li>Kills most vegetative pathogens, viruses, fungi and spores within 10 minutes</li>
<li>Some microbes resistant to boiling e.g. endospores (20 Hours)</li>
</ul>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="1-2-autoclave">1.2. Autoclave:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-2-autoclave" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Preferred method</li>
<li>Moist heat (steam) and pressure</li>
<li>Limitations: Material must be able withstand heat and moisture</li>
<li>15 psi (121 C) for 15 minutes will kill all organisms</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-7e12ddce.jpeg" alt="img-30.jpeg">
Autoclave</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-c8f7dd53.jpeg" alt="img-31.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="1-3-pasteurization-louis-pasteur">1.3. Pasteurization (Louis Pasteur):<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-3-pasteurization-louis-pasteur" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Mild heating (Initially 63 C for 30 minutes)</li>
<li>Kills most pathogens and bacteria that cause spoilage</li>
<li>Lowers bacterial numbers</li>
<li>Preserves taste of product</li>
<li>High temperature short  time pasteurization (HTST)</li>
<li>Kills pathogens</li>
<li>Lowers bacterial numbers, milk keeps while refrigerated</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="1-4-dry-heat-sterilization">1.4. Dry Heat Sterilization<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-4-dry-heat-sterilization" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Flaming</li>
<li>Incineration</li>
<li>Hot air sterilization: Placed in oven (170 C for 2 hours)</li>
</ul>
<p>31</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="2-filtration">2. Filtration:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-filtration" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Liquids and heat sensitive materials</li>
<li>Filters composed of cellulose or plastic polymers. Vacuum assists gravity</li>
<li>Small pores prevent passage of bacteria (0.1m -1mm)</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-74e1d406.jpeg" alt="img-32.jpeg"></p>
<p>Sterilization by filtration</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="3-low-temperatures-refrigeration">3. Low Temperatures (Refrigeration):<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-low-temperatures-refrigeration" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Bacteriostatic (stop microbial growth)</li>
<li>Psychrotrophs still present and grow</li>
<li>Slow freezing more harmful to bacteria than rapid.</li>
<li>Ice disrupts the cell structure</li>
<li>Thawing damages bacteria as well</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="4-high-pressure">4. High Pressure:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-high-pressure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Applied to liquid suspensions</li>
<li>Alters protein shape</li>
<li>Endospores are resistant</li>
<li>Can be killed by altering pressure cycles</li>
<li>Endospores germinate then exposed to pressure again</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="5">
<li>Desiccation:</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Removal of water</li>
<li>Microorganisms cannot grow but still survive</li>
<li>Re-introduce water microorganisms resume growth and division</li>
<li>Effectiveness varies between organisms</li>
<li>Neisseria withstand dryness for one hour</li>
<li>Mycoplasma withstand dryness for months</li>
<li>Endospores remain for centuries</li>
</ul>
<ol start="6">
<li>Osmotic Pressure:</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>High concentrations of salt and sugar</li>
<li>Creates hypertonic environment</li>
<li>Water leaves microbes cell</li>
<li>Molds and yeasts can grow better than bacteria in high osmotic pressure or low moisture</li>
</ul>
<ol start="7">
<li>Radiation:</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Ionizing radiation (gamma rays)</li>
<li>High energy short wavelength</li>
<li>Radioactive elements</li>
<li>X-rays</li>
<li>Penetrate deeply</li>
<li>Require longer times</li>
<li>Ionizes water to form hydroxyl radicals</li>
<li>Food preservation in other countries</li>
</ul>
<p>32</p>
<p>Chemical Methods of Microbial Growth Control:</p>
<p>Examples of some chemicals used for microbial growth control:</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>TABLE 7.8 Chemical Agents Used to Control Microbial Growth (continued)</th><th></th><th></th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Chemical Agent</td><td>Mechanism of Action</td><td>Preferred Use</td><td>Comment</td></tr><tr><td>Alcohols</td><td>Protein denaturation and lipid dissolution.</td><td>Thermometers and other instruments; in swabbing the skin with alcohol before an injection, most of the disinfecting action probably comes from a simple wiping away (degerming) of dirt and some microbes.</td><td>Bactericidal and fungicidal, but not effective against endospores or nonenveloped viruses; commonly used alcohols are ethanol and isopropanol.</td></tr><tr><td>Heavy Metals and Their Compounds</td><td>Denaturation of enzymes and other essential proteins.</td><td>Silver nitrate may be used to prevent gonorrheal ophthalmia neonatorum; mercurochrome disinfects skin and mucous membranes; copper sulfate is an algicide.</td><td>Heavy metals such as silver and mercury are biocidal.</td></tr><tr><td>Surface-Active Agents</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>1. Soaps and acid-anionic detergents</td><td>Mechanical removal of microbes through scrubbing.</td><td>Skin degerming and removal of debris.</td><td>Many antibacterial soaps contain antimicrobials.</td></tr><tr><td>2. Acid-anionic detergents</td><td>Not certain; may involve enzyme inactivation or disruption.</td><td>Sanitizers in dairy and food-processing industries.</td><td>Wide spectrum of activity; nontoxic, noncorrosive, fast-acting.</td></tr></tbody></table>
